Thus, they had preconceived notions of what it was like to work at a successful company. As at many companies, one of the consequences of wild success is the pernicious distortion of reality. Increasingly we would hear that people had opinions about things they thought were wrong but were unwilling to express them. One of our greatest values‚Äîthat solutions could come from anyone and that everyone should feel free to weigh in‚Äîwas slowly being subverted under our watchful eyes. And only we could correct it.&#13; ‚ÄúSometimes I think people have gotten too comfortable,‚Äù John said when we gathered in a renovated chapel on the resort grounds. ‚ÄúThey need to feel excited‚Äîto feel like we once did: on fire and buzzing with possibility!‚Äù&#13; This wasn‚Äôt the first time John and I had wondered about how Pixar‚Äôs people were affected by being at the front of the pack for so long. Would they gradually begin to take success for granted? ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs a lightness and a speed at Disney that I want to see more of at Pixar,‚Äù John said.&#13; How, we all wondered, could we maintain Pixar‚Äôs sense of intensity and playfulness, beating back the creeping conservatism that often accompanies success while also getting leaner and more nimble?&#13; That‚Äôs when Guido Quaroni spoke up. Guido is vice president of our Tools Department, and he spends a lot of time thinking about how to keep his 120 engineers happy. His challenge on that front is real: His department develops technology, but Pixar doesn‚Äôt sell technology. It sells stories enabled by technology. Which means that when a Pixar engineer develops a piece of software, it is deemed successful only insomuch as it helps our movies get made. I‚Äôve talked about the problem at Pixar of people questioning how much of each movie‚Äôs success can be attributed to them personally. For engineers, that uncertainty can be particularly acute. Guido knows that if he‚Äôs not careful, that disconnect can lead to low morale. So to retain the best engineers, he works extra hard to make sure they enjoy their jobs.&#13; When Guido had the floor, he told a story about something he‚Äôd instituted in his department called ‚Äúpersonal project days.‚Äù Two days a month, he allowed his engineers to work on anything they wanted, using Pixar‚Äôs resources to engage with whatever problem or question they found interesting. It didn‚Äôt have to be directly applicable to any particular film or address any of production‚Äôs needs. If an engineer wanted to see what it was like to light a shot in Brave, for example, he or she could. If a group of engineers wanted to build a prototype using Kinect, Microsoft‚Äôs motion-sensing input device, to help animators capture characters‚Äô movements, they could do that, too. Any idea that sparked their curiosity, they were free to pursue.&#13; ‚ÄúYou just give people the time, and they come up with the ideas,‚Äù Guido told us. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs the beauty of it: It comes from them.‚Äù&#13; Guido had already told me about how, in just four months, personal project days had reenergized his staff. Privately, we‚Äôd even begun to brainstorm ideas about how similar efforts could be implemented company-wide. At one point, he‚Äôd suggested shutting down Pixar for a week at the end of a movie‚Äôs production cycle to talk about what went right, what went wrong, and how to reboot for the next project‚Äîa sort of super-postmortem. The idea wasn‚Äôt practical, in the end, but it was thought-provoking. Now, as we contemplated how to achieve our goal of cutting costs by 10 percent, Guido had a simple suggestion.&#13; ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs ask Pixar‚Äôs people‚Äîall of them‚Äîfor ideas about how to do it,‚Äù he said.&#13; Looking at John, I could see his gears begin turning. ‚ÄúOkay, now that‚Äôs interesting,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúWhat if we closed Pixar for the day? Everybody will come to work but all we‚Äôll talk about is how to solve this problem. We dedicate an entire day to it.‚Äù&#13; The room was instantly abuzz. ‚ÄúThis is so Pixar,‚Äù Andrew said. ‚ÄúTotally unexpected. Yes! You want people to get excited? That‚Äôs going to do it.‚Äù&#13; When I asked who in the room would be willing to help organize it, everyone‚Äôs hands shot up.&#13; &#13; I believe that no creative company should ever stop evolving, and this would be our latest attempt to avoid stagnation. We wanted to explore issues big and small‚Äîto give candid notes to ourselves about the workings of the company, much like we would give notes on a movie in a Braintrust meeting. So it made sense, as we began to make Guido‚Äôs idea a reality, to invoke our shorthand term for candid feedback: notes. At some point, we decided that Monday, March 11, 2013, would be called ‚ÄúNotes Day.‚Äù&#13; The exercise would be fruitless without the buy-in of our people, so we scheduled three town hall‚Äìstyle meetings to explain the idea to more than 300 employees at a time. Tom Porter presented an abbreviated version of his off-site talk to set up the problem, and then John and I laid out the plan. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôll be a day in which you tell us how to make Pixar better,‚Äù John said. ‚ÄúWe‚Äôll do no work that day. No visitors will be allowed. Everyone must attend.‚Äù&#13; ‚ÄúWe have a problem,‚Äù I said, ‚Äúand we believe the only people who know what to do about it are you.‚Äù&#13; We appointed Tom to preside over Notes Day and make sure that it was more than merely a feel-good exercise. From the start, he made clear what Notes Day was‚Äîand wasn‚Äôt. ‚ÄúThis is not a call for working faster or doing more overtime or making do with fewer people,‚Äù he said in one town hall forum. ‚ÄúThis is about making three films every two years with roughly the same number of people we‚Äôve got today. We hope to rely on improvements in technology. We hope that productions can share resources and avoid reinventing the wheel each time. We hope that artists can benefit from greater clarity from the directors.‚Äù But to make good on these hopes‚Äîand to realize other areas in which we could improve‚ÄîPixar‚Äôs leaders needed everyone to speak up.&#13; Tom got together with Guido, Lori McAdams, the vice president of human resources, and producers Katherine Sarafian and Galyn Susman to form the core of the Notes Day Working Group. That group would soon expand, drafting dozens of volunteers for specific assignments. First, it created an electronic suggestion box where Pixar people could submit discussion topics they thought would help us become more innovative and more efficient. Immediately, topic ideas began flooding in, along with suggestions about how to run Notes Day itself.&#13; The suggestion box, in turn, prompted something that none of us had expected. Many departments, without any prodding, created their own wiki pages and blogs to hash out what they believed the core issues at Pixar really were. Weeks before Notes Day, people were talking among themselves in ways they hadn‚Äôt before about how, specifically, to improve workflow and enact positive change. When people asked for guidance on how to be involved, Tom nudged them along, sending this hypothetical prompt to anyone who asked: ‚ÄúThe year is 2017. Both of this year‚Äôs films were completed in well under 18,500 person-weeks.‚Ä¶ What innovations helped these productions meet their budget goals? What are some specific things that we did differently?‚Äù&#13; In the end, four thousand emails poured into the Notes Day suggestion box‚Äîcontaining one thousand separate ideas in all. As Tom and his team read and evaluated them, they were careful not to dismiss the unexpected. ‚ÄúWhile we discarded the ones that felt like general grumbling, we also made room for interesting ideas that might, but might not, lead somewhere,‚Äù he told me. ‚ÄúI am sure we were biased toward ideas that would clearly help us get to 18,500 person-weeks, but there were many topics we picked with only a loose or non-obvious connection to that goal. I‚Äôd say our major criterion was, ‚ÄòCan you imagine twenty people talking about this topic for an hour?‚Äô ‚Äù&#13; Putting like with like, Tom‚Äôs team distilled the thousand ideas down to 293 discussion topics. That was still way too many for a single day‚Äôs agenda, so a group of senior managers then met and whittled those down to 120 topics, organized into several broad categories such as Training, Environment and Culture; Cross-Show Resource Pooling (we often call our movies ‚Äúshows‚Äù); Tools and Technology; and Workflow.&#13; The winnowing process was difficult, and it was made even more so by the diversity of the questions posed. Some were valid but highly technical in nature, such as, ‚ÄúOut-of-memory errors related to inadequately pruned sets consume a significant amount of computer and human time. What can be done to improve pruning?‚Äù Others were more sociological, as in, ‚ÄúHow can we return to a ‚Äògood ideas come from anywhere‚Äô culture?‚Äù And then there was my favorite: ‚ÄúHow can we get to a 12,000 person-week movie?‚Äù That‚Äôs right: 12,000. This was a discussion topic prompted by emails from several people whose reaction to the call for a 10 percent budget cut was, naturally, to ask whether a more drastic cut might be possible as well.&#13; ‚ÄúEighteen Five, Smaiteen Five,‚Äù said the header on one email received by the Notes Day Working Group. What, this writer asked, if of the three films Pixar made every two years, one of them was produced for a ‚Äúreduced scope‚Äù of 15,000 person-weeks? Or even 12,500? ‚ÄúNo skimping on story, just simplifying the rest?‚Äù&#13; Another person emailed: ‚ÄúI, for one, would like to work on a ‚Äò10,000 person-week film.‚Äô I feel that the measures you‚Äôd design to enable it would inform efforts to make the 18,500 person-week film.‚Äù&#13; Still another asked: ‚ÄúWhat kind of film would Pixar make with 12,000 person-weeks? Is there a creative idea that could live up to our reputation but be done for that little? Where would the cuts be? What would be different about the process?‚Äù The subject line for that email, by the way, was ‚ÄúGET RADICAL.‚Äù&#13; Once the whittling process was complete, Tom needed to find out roughly how many people were interested in each discussion topic so that he could plan the day accordingly. To that end, the Notes Day Working Group circulated a survey, and what he learned was striking: The number one topic‚Äîthe one that the most people wanted to talk about‚Äîwas how to achieve a 12,000 person-week movie. In the end, Tom and his team would arrange seven separate 90-minute sessions on this topic alone. The people who signed up for these sessions weren‚Äôt martyrs. The problem of doing more with less was interesting to them, and they wanted to engage with it. (Think about that‚Äîthe topic that captured my Pixar colleagues‚Äô imagination more than any other was an attempt to be even more aggressive in trying to reduce the budget! They truly understood the problem and its implications. Can you see why I have so much pride in this place?)&#13; The nitty-gritty of how all this was organized may seem a bit micro to describe here, but it couldn‚Äôt have been more vital to the way the day played out. It‚Äôs all well and good to gather people to discuss workplace challenges, but it was extremely important that we find a way to turn all that talk into something tangible, usable, valuable.&#13; How the day was designed, we felt, would be the deciding factor in accomplishing that.&#13; Tom and his team decided early on that people would determine their own schedules, signing up for only the sessions that interested them. Each of the Notes Day discussion groups would be led by a facilitator recruited from among the company‚Äôs production managers. The week before Notes Day, all facilitators attended a training session to help them keep each meeting on track and make sure that everyone‚Äîthe outgoing, the laid-back, and everyone in between‚Äîwas heard from. Then, to make sure something concrete emerged, the Working Group designed a set of ‚Äúexit forms‚Äù to be filled out by each session‚Äôs participants.&#13; Red forms were for proposals, blue forms were for brainstorms, and yellow forms were for something we called ‚Äúbest practices‚Äù‚Äîideas that were not action items per se but principles about how we should behave as a company. The forms were simple and specific: Each session got its own set, tailored specifically to the topic at hand, that asked a specific question. For example, the session called ‚ÄúReturning to a ‚ÄòGood Ideas Come from Anywhere‚Äô Culture,‚Äù had blue exit forms topped with this header: Imagine it‚Äôs 2017. We‚Äôve broken down barriers so that people feel safe to speak up. Senior employees are open to new processes. What did we do to achieve this success? Underneath that question were boxes in which attendees could pencil in three answers. Then, after they wrote a general description of each idea, they were asked to go a few steps further. What ‚ÄúBenefits to Pixar‚Äù would these ideas bring? And what should be the ‚ÄúNext Steps‚Äù to make them a reality? Finally, there was space provided to specify ‚ÄúWho is the best audience for this idea?‚Äù and ‚ÄúWho should pitch this idea?‚Äù&#13; The goal was meaningful engagement that would lead to action. And while Tom and his team had made room for a variety of topics, there was a consistency to the way they were framed. A best practices session called ‚ÄúLessons from the Outside‚Äù had a yellow exit form that posed the question, ‚ÄúWhat can we learn from best practices at other companies?‚Äù Underneath, it had space for three lessons, each with the same ‚ÄúBenefits to Pixar/Next Steps‚Äù follow-up.&#13; The red exit form for a proposals session called ‚ÄúHelping Directors Understand Costs in Story‚Äù gave the session‚Äôs attendees a jumping-off point: Introduce the concept of cost early in the story process. Build in scope discussions in the idea-generation phase. Story plays a role in the budget process when building reels. Then, in a space marked ‚ÄúRevised Proposal?‚Äù this form encouraged participants to improve on the stated approach. ‚ÄúHow does this benefit the studio?‚Äù the form asked, and ‚ÄúWhat are the drawbacks?‚Äù At the bottom was another question, ‚ÄúIs This Idea Worth Pursuing?‚Äù with two boxes underneath: ‚ÄúYES! &amp; Next Steps‚Äù or ‚ÄúNO, because.‚Ä¶ ‚Äù The yes option asked: ‚ÄúWho‚Äôs the best audience for this proposal? (Be specific).‚Äù And again there was this: ‚ÄúWho should pitch this proposal?‚Äù&#13; I think you‚Äôre getting a sense of how hard our team worked to make sure Notes Day took us where we needed to go. As Tom put it, ‚ÄúWe didn‚Äôt just want to make lists of cool things we could do. The goal was to identify passionate people who would take ideas forward. We wanted to put people with clever insights in front of Pixar‚Äôs executive team.‚Äù&#13; On the Friday before Notes Day, I got an email telling me that 1,059 people had signed up‚Äînearly everyone in the company, given that some employees were on leave or away. The following Monday, we would discuss 106 topics in 171 sessions managed by 138 facilitators in 66 meeting spaces across our three buildings‚Äîfrom offices to conference rooms to common spaces like the Poodle Lounge, which has a painting of George Washington on the wall, a bean bag toss game on the floor, and a disco ball hanging overhead.&#13; We were as ready as we were ever going to be to let this experiment unfold.&#13; At 9 A.M. on March 11, everyone gathered in the atrium of the Steve Jobs Building. If the navy blue Pixar sweatshirt I was wearing didn‚Äôt make it obvious enough, the look on my face gave it away: I was enormously proud of how our people had already shown their commitment to making Notes Day a historic day for us. I told them as much as I welcomed them, and then I handed the mike to John.&#13; John often plays the role of inspirer-in-chief, and the people at Disney and Pixar alike rely on his energy and optimism. But this was no rah-rah call to action. Ambling to the front of the stage, John proceeded to deliver the most heartfelt and emotional speech I had ever heard him give. He started by talking about candor, and how we spend a lot of time at Pixar talking about its importance. But candor is hard, both to deliver and to receive. He knew this firsthand, he said, because in preparation for Notes Day, the organizers had shared something else that had come in to the electronic suggestion box: A fair amount of feedback had focused on John himself, and not all of it was positive. In particular, people were upset that‚Äîbecause he was now splitting his time between two studios‚Äîthey were seeing less of him. The bottom line was that they missed him, but they also felt that there were ways that John could better handle the inordinate pressure he was under.&#13; John admitted that this hurt; still, he wanted to hear all of the specific criticisms. ‚ÄúSo they prepared a list,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI thought it would be a page. Instead, I got two-and-a-half pages.‚Äù Among the things he learned: John was so tightly scheduled, and meetings with him were so precious, that people tended to overprepare to see him, which served no one. In fact, John said, ‚Äúthere were a lot of notes about my time management, and how I carry the emotion of one meeting into the next, making some people ask, ‚ÄòWhy is he upset at us?‚Äô I didn‚Äôt know I was doing any of this, and those two-and-a-half pages were really tough to read. But it was so valuable for me to hear, and I‚Äôm already working to correct those things.‚Äù&#13; The atrium was quiet, despite the crowd.&#13; ‚ÄúSo, today, please be honest,‚Äù John continued. ‚ÄúAnd those of you in management positions, be aware that some of this is going to feel like it‚Äôs directed at you personally. I‚Äôm not kidding. It‚Äôs going to happen. But put your tough skin on, and for the sake of Pixar, speak up, and don‚Äôt stop the honesty. Trust me. That‚Äôs what today is about. It‚Äôs about making Pixar better forever, for all of you and for the next generations of Pixarians. This is going to fundamentally change the company for the better. But it starts with you.‚Äù&#13; It was time to go to class.&#13; For the first hour of Notes Day, everyone at the company headed to their own departmental meeting‚ÄîStory, Lighting, Shading, Accounting, what have you‚Äîwhere they shared ideas with their closest colleagues about how to be more efficient. These departmental meetings, we felt, would serve as a sort of warm-up for the day; it‚Äôs always easier to be candid with people you know than with strangers. But as John had urged, Pixar‚Äôs people needed to put their thickest skins and bravest faces on. Because beginning at 10:45 A.M., when everybody headed to their first session, chances were good that for the rest of the day, no Pixar employee was going to find him- or herself sitting next to any of the people they knew best.&#13; Why? Because the sessions weren‚Äôt organized by job or by department. They were organized by individual interest. During the lead-up to Notes Day, each person had been asked what they wanted to discuss, and Tom‚Äôs team had created enough sessions to accommodate everyone. While some topics were so specialized that interest was limited to a narrow subset of employees (for example, to take just one: ‚ÄúWhat range of solutions do we have for improving Lighting productivity?‚Äù), curiosity being what it is, many topics attracted all kinds of people from across the company.&#13; If you showed up, for instance, to a brainstorming session called ‚ÄúDeveloping and Appreciating a Great Workplace‚Äù‚ÄîIt‚Äôs 2017. Nobody at the studio behaves as if they are entitled. How did we accomplish that?‚Äîyou would have found Pixar‚Äôs executive chef, a woman who worked in Legal, a woman from Finance, a veteran animator, a man from Systems, and more than a dozen others. What had attracted such a cross-section? For that particular session, everyone said they picked it because of the word entitled in the descriptor. They‚Äôd all encountered people who acted entitled at Pixar‚Äîpeople who insisted on having their own piece of equipment, even if it could be shared, or who groused that they couldn‚Äôt bring their dogs to work. ‚ÄúThis is a job,‚Äù one animator said. ‚ÄúA great job. We are well paid. These people need to wake up.‚Äù&#13; What was most striking to those in attendance at the ‚ÄúGreat Workplace‚Äù session was how much they had in common. The Systems guy told a story about answering a frantic call for tech support. He rushed over to assess the problem, only to be told by the aggrieved artist that the machine should be fixed during lunch‚Äîbecause that‚Äôs when it would be most convenient for her. ‚ÄúI need to eat lunch, too,‚Äù he told the group, as everyone nodded their heads. The chef told a similar story about a last-minute request to cater a working lunch that came without any acknowledgement of the hassle (and hustle) it would require of her staff. A character animator lamented that he didn‚Äôt know more about what people in other departments, like lighting and shading, did. ‚ÄúIt makes it easy to vilify and resent each other,‚Äù he said.&#13; One by one, the people in this session hit on the same themes. ‚ÄúWe need to make people behave more like peers,‚Äù one person said. ‚ÄúI wish more people knew about the whole production pipeline‚Äîby which I mean, that they appreciated and understood what other people do,‚Äù said another. ‚ÄúWe need to heighten people‚Äôs awareness of what they do not know.‚Äù&#13; Among the ideas this group put on their exit forms: fostering more empathy between departments through a job-swapping program, establishing a lunch lottery that would match people at random to encourage new connections and friendships, and holding cross-departmental mixers designed to let far-flung colleagues get to know each other over a few beers.&#13; I chose to describe that session in part because it‚Äôs broadly relatable‚Äîno matter what business you‚Äôre in, you‚Äôve run across the scourge of entitlement. (Were I to describe some other Notes Day sessions‚Äîone on centralized rendering, say‚ÄîI think I‚Äôd risk losing a few people.) But regardless of the topic that was being discussed, no matter where you were on campus, you could feel a frisson of energy. If you stepped into a Pixar restroom or stepped outside for some air, you couldn‚Äôt avoid overhearing people chatting about how exciting Notes Day was. The feeling was that we were engaged in something that would make a difference.&#13; Midway through the day, Tom gathered the facilitators to check, briefly, how things were going and to encourage them to share their experiences thus far. At one point, he asked, ‚ÄúHow many of you had suggestions in your sessions that could be implemented immediately?‚Äù Everybody raised their hands.&#13; We‚Äôd made a decision to separate out Pixar‚Äôs executives, directors, and producers from the Notes Day sessions. Partly this was because it was vital that people speak freely, and we weren‚Äôt sure they would if we were there. Partly, too, we peeled off because there were particular topics that we needed to consider among ourselves: creative oversight (Are Braintrust sessions as useful as they were ten years ago?), leadership tone and temperament (How can we better foster a culture of inclusiveness in which anyone can suggest a labor-saving idea?), the need to spend money where it can do the most good (We have a system that is vulnerable to excess, that rewards perfectionists and pleasers. How do we manage perfectionism and the desire to innovate?).&#13; I knew things were going well from the looks on our colleagues‚Äô faces as they hurried from session to session. They were beaming. At day‚Äôs end, as the entire company gathered outside for beer, hot dogs, and some instant analysis, I noticed people from different departments continuing the discussions they‚Äôd begun inside. The energy on the whole campus was electric. This was the Pixar that they wanted, that we wanted. I made a point of stopping by several bulletin boards we‚Äôd erected to encourage people to share their impressions. Among those posted under a variety of categories were:&#13; Favorite moment from Notes Day: ‚ÄúJohn Lasseter‚Äôs candor.‚Äù&#13; Something new I learned today: ‚ÄúPeople care; people can change.‚Äù&#13; How many new people did you meet today? ‚Äú23.‚Äù&#13; And then there was this: ‚ÄúNotes Day is the proof that Pixar cares about people as much as about finances.‚Äù And: ‚ÄúDo this again next year.‚Äù&#13; The next morning, I received emails from hundreds of employees. One, from a storyboard artist, perfectly captured the feeling expressed by many. ‚ÄúHello Ed,‚Äù it read. ‚ÄúI just wanted to say a post‚ÄìNotes Day thank you. The day was truly amazing, inspirational, informative and as I heard many times throughout the day, from many people, cathartic. If there was any cynicism anywhere, I didn‚Äôt see it. Coming away from it, I felt as though the company shrank a little. I met new people, got completely new points of view, and learned what other departments struggle with, and succeed with. I don‚Äôt know if a metric exists to measure the impact of Notes Day, but from where I was standing, it was huge. In the end, I think we all walked away with a sense of ownership over this amazing place, and its future. A ‚Äòwe‚Äôre all in this together‚Äô feel. If nothing else, this is a huge victory. John‚Äôs openness, and courage to speak about his feedback, set an unbelievable bar. His admission put the entire company firmly behind him, and was one of the finest instances of ‚Äòleading by example‚Äô I can think of. I think we can all learn from that and accept our own introspection/feedback with a similar grace and humility. Thank you so much for creating an environment where this kind of discussion can happen.‚Äù&#13; You‚Äôll remember that the exit forms filled out by Notes Day participants weren‚Äôt shy about asking, ‚ÄúWho should pitch this proposal?‚Äù That was by design‚Äîwe wanted the best ideas to be pushed forward, not to languish. So in the weeks after Notes Day, all those who‚Äôd volunteered to be ‚Äúidea advocates‚Äù were called in to work with Tom and his team to hone their pitches. Then, they began making them to me, John, and our general manager, Jim Morris‚Äîand together, we immediately began moving to implement the ones that made sense.&#13; The ideas that emerged on Notes Day, in other words, were not gathering dust in a drawer. They were changing Pixar‚Äîmeaningfully and for the better. The specific procedural changes will sound mundane to anyone who doesn‚Äôt work in animation‚Äîwe implemented a faster, more secure way, to cite a tiny example, of delivering the latest cuts of films to directors‚Äîbut when you add them all up, they mattered. In the weeks after Notes Day, we implemented four good ideas, committed to five more, and earmarked still a dozen more for continued development. All of them stood to improve either our processes, our culture, or the way Pixar is managed.&#13; Most importantly, though, we broke the logjam that was getting in the way of candor and making it feel dangerous. Some people might measure the day‚Äôs success by charting the concrete results that resulted from it, and in fact, we have paid attention to that too. But real improvement comes from consistent rigor and participation. For this reason, I believe the biggest payoff of Notes Day was that we made it safer for people to say what they thought. Notes Day made it okay to disagree. That and the feeling our people had that they were part of the solution were its biggest contributions.&#13; What made Notes Day work? To me, it boils down to three factors. First, there was a clear and focused goal. This wasn‚Äôt a free-for-all but a wide-ranging discussion (organized around topics suggested not by Human Resources or by Pixar‚Äôs executives, but by the company‚Äôs employees) aimed at addressing a specific reality: the need to cut our costs by 10 percent. While the discussion topics were allowed‚Äîeven encouraged‚Äîto stray into areas that might seem only vaguely related to this goal, the fact that it was there was key. It provided a framework‚Äîand it kept us from falling into confusion.&#13; Second, this was an idea championed by those at the highest levels of the company. Had the enormous task of making Notes Day a reality been shunted off on someone who didn‚Äôt have the clout to throw muscle behind it‚Äîand not entrusted to Tom, who in turn recruited the most organized people in the company to help him‚Äîit would have been an entirely different experience. Employees wouldn‚Äôt have bought into the idea because they‚Äôd sense that management hadn‚Äôt, either. And that would have rendered Notes Day moot.&#13; Third, and relatedly, Notes Day was led from within. Many companies hire outside consulting firms to organize their all-staff retreats, and I understand why: Doing them well is a monumental, enormously time-consuming undertaking. But that our own people made Notes Day happen was, I believe, key to its success. Not only did they drive the discussion in meaningful ways, but their involvement also paid its own dividends. Seeing themselves engage and cooperate, steering the agenda toward something that could make a real difference, they remembered why they worked at Pixar. Their commitment was contagious. Notes Day wasn‚Äôt an end point but a beginning‚Äîa way of making room for our employees to step forward and think about their role in our company‚Äôs future. I said before that problems are easy to identify, but finding the source of those problems is extraordinarily difficult. Notes brought problems to the surface‚Äîbut we still had the hard work in front of us. Notes Day didn‚Äôt solve anything all by itself. But it shifted our culture‚Äîrepaired it, even‚Äîin ways that will make us better as we go forward.&#13; I‚Äôve said it before, but it bears repeating: Things change, constantly, as they should. And with change comes the need for adaptation, for fresh thinking, and, sometimes, for even a total reboot‚Äîof your project, your department, your division, or your company as a whole. In times of change, we need support‚Äîfrom our families and from our colleagues. I‚Äôm reminded here of a letter written by one of our animators, Austin Madison, which I found particularly uplifting.&#13; ‚ÄúTo Whom it May Inspire,‚Äù Austin wrote. ‚ÄúI, like many of you artists out there, constantly shift between two states. The first (and far more preferable of the two) is white-hot, ‚Äòin the zone‚Äô seat-of-the-pants, firing on all cylinders creative mode. This is when you lay your pen down and the ideas pour out like wine from a royal chalice! This happens about 3% of the time. The other 97% of the time I am in the frustrated, struggling, office-corner-full-of-crumpled-up-paper mode. The important thing is to slog diligently through this quagmire of discouragement and despair. Put on some audio commentary and listen to the stories of professionals who have been making films for decades going through the same slings and arrows of outrageous production problems. In a word: PERSIST. PERSIST on telling your story. PERSIST on reaching your audience. PERSIST on staying true to your vision.‚Ä¶ ‚Äù&#13; I couldn‚Äôt have put it any better. My goal has never been to tell people how Pixar and Disney figured it all out but rather to show how we continue to figure it out, every hour of every day. How we persist. The future is not a destination‚Äîit is a direction. It is our job, then, to work each day to chart the right course and make corrections when, inevitably, we stray. I already can sense the next crisis coming around the corner. To keep a creative culture vibrant, we must not be afraid of constant uncertainty. We must accept it, just as we accept the weather. Uncertainty and change are life‚Äôs constants. And that‚Äôs the fun part.&#13; The truth is, as challenges emerge, mistakes will always be made, and our work is never done. We will always have problems, many of which are hidden from our view; we must work to uncover them and assess our own role in them, even if doing so means making ourselves uncomfortable; when we then come across a problem, we must marshal all our energies to solve it. If those assertions sound familiar, that‚Äôs because I used them to kick off this book. There‚Äôs something else that bears repeating here: Unleashing creativity requires that we loosen the controls, accept risk, trust our colleagues, work to clear the path for them, and pay attention to anything that creates fear. Doing all these things won‚Äôt necessarily make the job of managing a creative culture easier. But ease isn‚Äôt the goal; excellence is.&#13;

 AFTERWORD&#13; &#13; THE STEVE WE KNEW&#13; It was the end of 1985, and the computer division I ran at Lucasfilm was short on suitors and, it seemed, out of options. Our tires had been kicked by anyone with even the slightest interest in computer-generated imaging. We‚Äôd made a promising match with General Motors, only to be left at the altar. Then Steve Jobs swooped in. As I related earlier, it was around this time that one of his attorneys pulled us aside during a meeting and jokingly‚ÄîI think‚Äîsaid that we were about to climb aboard the Steve Jobs roller coaster. Get on, we did, and what a ride it would prove to be‚Äîwith all of the attendant ups and downs.&#13; I worked closely with Steve Jobs for twenty-six years. To this day, for all that has been written about him, I don‚Äôt believe that any of it comes close to capturing the man I knew. I‚Äôve been frustrated that the stories about him tend to focus so narrowly on his extreme traits and the negative, difficult aspects of his personality. Inevitably, profiles of Steve describe him as stubborn and imperious, a man who held steadfastly and unwaveringly to his own ideals, refusing to budge or change, and who often tried to browbeat others into doing things his way. While many of the anecdotes people repeat about his behavior as a young executive are probably accurate, the overall portrait is way off the mark. The reality is, Steve changed profoundly in the years that I knew him.&#13; The word genius is used a lot these days‚Äîtoo much, I think‚Äîbut with Steve, I actually think it was warranted. Still, when I first came to know him, he was frequently dismissive and brusque. This is the part of Steve that people love to write about. I realize that it is difficult to understand people who deviate so radically from the norm, like Steve did, and I suspect that those who focus on his more extreme traits do so because those traits are entertaining and revealing in some way. To let them drive Steve‚Äôs narrative, however, is to miss the more important story. In the time I worked with Steve, he didn‚Äôt just gain the kind of practical experience you would expect to acquire while running two dynamic, successful businesses; he also got smarter about when to stop pushing people and how to keep pushing them, if necessary, without breaking them. He became fairer and wiser, and his understanding of partnership deepened‚Äîin large part because of his marriage to Laurene and his relationships with the children he loved so much. This shift didn‚Äôt lead him to abandon his famous commitment to innovation; it solidified it. At the same time, he developed into a kinder, more self-aware leader. And I think Pixar played a role in that development.&#13; Remember, in the late 1980s, when Pixar was founded, Steve was spending most of his time building NeXT, the computer company he‚Äôd started after being forced out of Apple. At Pixar, none of us, including Steve, knew what we were doing. Steve would overreach in early negotiations with customers, which sometimes worked but sometimes backfired. At NeXT, for instance, he struck a $100 million deal that allowed IBM to use the NeXT software. The huge dollar amount, combined with the fact that Steve didn‚Äôt give IBM rights to subsequent versions of the software, seemed like a home run deal for NeXT. In fact, Steve had overreached‚Äîhis behavior created ill will, and he later told me he learned from that.&#13; In those early days, Steve sensed that there was something quite special going on at Pixar, but it frustrated him that he couldn‚Äôt figure it out‚Äîand kept losing money in the meantime. He had an expensive group that was ahead of its time. Could he hang on long enough for that potential to flower, especially if he didn‚Äôt know if it ever would flower? What kind of person signs on for that? Would you?&#13; We tend to think of emotion and logic as two distinct, mutually exclusive domains. Not Steve. From the beginning, when making decisions, passion was a key part of his calculus. At first, he often elicited it in a ham-handed way, by making extreme or outrageous statements and challenging people to respond. But at Pixar, even when we were a long way from being in the black, that aggressiveness was tempered by his acknowledgment that we knew things about graphics and storytelling that he did not. He respected our determination to be the first to make a computer-animated feature film. He didn‚Äôt tell us how to do our work or come in and impose his will. Even when we were unsure how to reach our goal, our passion was something Steve recognized and valued. That‚Äôs what Steve, John, and I ultimately bonded over: passion for excellence‚Äîa passion so ardent we were willing to argue and struggle and stay together, even when things got extremely uncomfortable.&#13; I remember being struck by Steve‚Äôs response to passion when we were working on our second film, A Bug‚Äôs Life. There was an internal disagreement about the aspect ratio of the film‚Äîthe proportional relationship between its width and its height. In a movie theater, films are displayed in widescreen format, where the width of the picture is more than twice the height; on the TVs of that time, by contrast, the width of the picture was only one and one-third times the height, more of a boxy shape. When you make a video version of a widescreen film that will be viewed on a TV monitor, then, you either end up with black bars at the top and bottom of the screen, or you clip off the sides of the picture completely, neither of which is a good representation of the original film.&#13; On A Bug‚Äôs Life, the marketing people were in conflict with the filmmakers. The filmmakers wanted the widescreen format because it led to a better panoramic experience in the theater, which they believed to be more important than the home viewing experience. The marketers, believing that consumers were less likely to buy a video with black bars on the top and bottom, argued that the widescreen format would mean a reduction in our DVD sales. Steve‚Äîno film buff‚Äîagreed with the marketing people that we would be hurting ourselves financially if we released the movie in widescreen. The debate about this was still unresolved when, one afternoon, I took Steve around the offices so he could see some of Pixar‚Äôs departments in action, and we ended up in a room full of people who were working on lighting a scene from A Bug‚Äôs Life. The production designer on the film, Bill Cone, was showing some images on monitors that happened to be in widescreen format.&#13; Seeing this, Steve interjected, in his way, that it was ‚Äúnuts‚Äù for us to be making a widescreen movie. Bill, to his credit, came right back at him, explaining why the widescreen format was absolutely crucial from an artistic standpoint. An intense back-and-forth followed. I wouldn‚Äôt call it an argument, but it was definitely heated. The discussion seemed to end inconclusively, and Steve and I continued on our rounds.&#13; Later, Bill came to see me, looking rattled. ‚ÄúOh, my God,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúI was just arguing with Steve Jobs. Did I blow it?‚Äù&#13; ‚ÄúOn the contrary,‚Äù I told him. ‚ÄúYou won.‚Äù&#13; I could see something that Bill couldn‚Äôt: Steve had responded to Bill‚Äôs passion about the issue. The fact that Bill was willing to stand up so forcefully and articulately for what he believed showed Steve that Bill‚Äôs ideas were worthy of respect. Steve never raised the format issue with us again.&#13; It wasn‚Äôt that passion trumped logic in Steve‚Äôs mind. He was well aware that decisions must never be based on emotions alone. But he also saw that creativity wasn‚Äôt linear, that art was not commerce, and that to insist upon applying dollars-and-cents logic was to risk disrupting the thing that set us apart. Steve put a premium on both sides of this equation, logic and emotion, and the way he maintained that balance was key to understanding him.&#13; In the mid-1990s, it became clear that Pixar, long housed in a few cramped, tilt-up buildings in Point Richmond, California, was going to need a new home. The time had come to establish a proper headquarters‚Äîa place of our own, suited to our needs. Steve threw himself into designing it, and the magnificent main building that we occupy today is the outgrowth of all that work. But it didn‚Äôt come easily.&#13; Steve‚Äôs first pass at a design was based on some peculiar ideas he had about how to force interaction among people. At an off-site staff meeting to discuss these plans in 1998, several people rose to complain about his intent to build a single women‚Äôs and a single men‚Äôs restroom. Steve relented, but he was clearly frustrated that people didn‚Äôt understand what he was trying to do: Bring people together out of necessity. At first, Steve struggled to find the best way to enable that mutual experience.&#13; Next, he envisioned a separate building for each movie under production‚Äîthe idea being that each crew would benefit from having its own contained space, free of distraction. I wasn‚Äôt so sure about that, so I asked him to go on a road trip.&#13; Showing, not telling, worked best with Steve, which is why I coaxed him south to Burbank for a tour of the four-story glazed-glass-and-aluminum building on Thornton Avenue known as Northside. Disney Animation had taken it over in 1997, using it to house the crew for its first 3D animated movie, Dinosaur, among other projects.&#13; But the building was more famous for what it had housed in the 1940s: Lockheed‚Äôs top-secret Skunk Works division, which designed jet fighters, spy planes, and at least one stealth fighter. I loved that bit of history‚Äîand the fact that the name Skunk Works itself had been borrowed from Al Capp‚Äôs newspaper comic strip Li‚Äôl Abner. In that strip, there was a running joke about a mysterious and malodorous place deep in the forest called the ‚ÄúSkonk Works‚Äù where a strong beverage was brewed from skunks, old shoes, and other strange ingredients.&#13; Steve knew that my purpose that day wasn‚Äôt to discuss comic strips or aviation history but to show him the building‚Äîa welcoming space where several hundred animators worked on multiple projects simultaneously, under a single roof. I liked the feel of the wide-open hallways. I recall Steve being critical of numerous facets of the building‚Äôs layout, but after an hour or so wandering around the place, I could tell he was getting the message: Creating separate buildings for each film would be isolating. He saw firsthand the way that the Disney people took advantage of the open floor plan, sharing information and brainstorming. Steve was a big believer in the power of accidental mingling; he knew that creativity was not a solitary endeavor. But our trip to Northside helped clarify that thinking. In a creative company, separating your people into distinct silos‚ÄîProject A over here, Project B over there‚Äîcan be counterproductive.&#13; After that trip, he met again with his architects and laid out the principles for a single building. He took the creation of a new Pixar headquarters as a personal responsibility.&#13; You‚Äôve heard the saying ‚ÄúYour employees are your most important asset.‚Äù For most executives, these are just words you trot out to make people feel good‚Äîwhile they may be accepted as true, few leaders alter their behavior or make decisions based upon them. But Steve did, taking that principle and building our headquarters around it. Everything about the place was designed to encourage people to mingle, meet, and communicate, to support our filmmaking by enhancing our ability to work together.&#13; In the end, Steve presided over every detail of our new building‚Äôs construction, from the arched steel bridges that straddle the central atrium to the type of chairs in our screening rooms. He didn‚Äôt want perceived barriers, so the stairs were open and inviting. He wanted a single entrance to the building so that we saw each other as we entered. We had meeting rooms, restrooms, a mailroom, three theaters, a game area, and an eating area all at the center in our atrium (where to this day, everyone gathers to eat, play ping pong, or be briefed by Pixar‚Äôs leaders on the company‚Äôs goings on). This all resulted in cross-traffic‚Äîpeople encountered each other all day long, inadvertently, which meant a better flow of communication and increased the possibility of chance encounters. You felt the energy in the building. Steve had thought all this through with the metalogic of a philosopher and the meticulousness of a craftsman. He believed in simple materials, masterfully constructed. He wanted all the steel exposed, not painted. He wanted glass doors to be flush with the walls. No wonder that when it opened in the fall of 2000, after four years of planning and construction, Pixar‚Äôs people‚Äîwho typically worked for four years on each film‚Äîtook to calling the building ‚ÄúSteve‚Äôs movie.‚Äù&#13; I admit that there were moments when I worried that Pixar would fall prey to the ‚Äúedifice complex,‚Äù wherein companies build shiny headquarters that are mere extensions of executive ego. But that worry proved unfounded. From the day we moved in, on Thanksgiving weekend of 2000, the building became an extraordinary and fertile home. Moreover, in our employees‚Äô minds, it transformed Steve‚Äîalways our external defender‚Äîinto an integral part of our internal culture. The environment was so exemplary and so clearly attributed to Steve that everyone could appreciate his singular contribution to‚Äîand understanding of‚Äîthe way we worked.&#13; That appreciation was a positive development because, as I‚Äôve said before, upon meeting Steve, people typically had to become accustomed to his style. Brad Bird remembers a meeting during the making of The Incredibles, soon after he joined the studio, when Steve hurt his feelings by saying that some of the Incredibles artwork looked ‚Äúkind of Saturday morning‚Äù‚Äîa reference to the low-budget cartoons that Hanna-Barbera and others produced. ‚ÄúIn my world, that‚Äôs kind of like saying, ‚ÄòYour mama sleeps around,‚Äô ‚Äù Brad recalls. ‚ÄúI was seething. When the meeting ended, I went over to Andrew and said, ‚ÄòMan, Steve just said something that really pissed me off.‚Äô And Andrew, without even asking what it was, said, ‚ÄòOnly one thing?‚Äô ‚Äù Brad came to understand that Steve was speaking not as a critic but as the ultimate advocate. Too often, animated superheroes had been made on the cheap and looked that way, too‚Äîon that Steve and Brad could agree. The Incredibles, he was implying, had to reach higher. ‚ÄúHe was just saying that we have to show this is something bigger,‚Äù Brad says. ‚ÄúAnd that epitomized Steve.‚Äù&#13; Though no one outside Pixar knew it, Steve developed a lasting bond with our directors. At first I thought this was just because he appreciated their creative and leadership abilities and they, in turn, appreciated the support and insight he gave them. But as I paid closer attention, I recognized there was something very important that they shared. When directors pitch an idea, for example, they invest totally, even though a part of them knows that in the end, it may not work at all. Pitching is a way of testing material, taking its measure‚Äîand, importantly, strengthening it‚Äîby observing how it plays to an audience. But if the idea doesn‚Äôt fly, they are extremely adept at dropping it and moving on. This is a rare skill, one that Steve had too.&#13; Steve had a remarkable knack for letting go of things that didn‚Äôt work. If you were in an argument with him, and you convinced him that you were right, he would instantly change his mind. He didn‚Äôt hold on to an idea because he had once believed it to be brilliant. His ego didn‚Äôt attach to the suggestions he made, even as he threw his full weight behind them. When Steve saw Pixar‚Äôs directors do the same, he recognized them as kindred spirits.&#13; One of the dangers of this approach can be that if you are pitching intently, your very exuberance can make others reluctant to respond candidly. When someone has a strong personality, others can wilt in the face of their intensity. How do you prevent this from happening? The trick is to shift the emphasis in any meeting away from the source of an idea and onto the idea itself. People often place too much significance on the source of an idea, accepting it (or not criticizing it) because it comes from Steve or a respected director. But Steve had no interest in that kind of affirmation. Countless times, I remember watching him toss ideas‚Äîpretty far-out ideas‚Äîinto the air, just to see how they played. And if they didn‚Äôt play well, he would move on. This is, in effect, a form of storytelling‚Äîsearching for the best way to frame and communicate an idea. If people didn‚Äôt understand Steve, they would misinterpret his floating of ideas as advocacy. And they would wrongly perceive his enthusiasm or insistence as intransigence or bullheadedness. Instead, he was gauging reactions to his ideas to see whether or not he should become their advocate.&#13; Steve is not commonly described as a storyteller, and he was always careful to say he didn‚Äôt know the first thing about filmmaking. Yet part of his bond with our directors stemmed from the fact that he knew how important it was to construct a story that connected with people. This was a skill he used in his presentations at Apple. When he got up in front of an audience to introduce a new product, he understood that he would communicate more effectively if he put forward a narrative, and anyone who ever saw him do it could tell you that he gave extraordinary and carefully crafted performances.&#13; At Pixar, Steve was able to participate in other people‚Äôs crafting of their stories, and I believe this process helped him understand more about human dynamics. There was something about applying his intellect to the emotion of a film‚ÄîWas it landing? Did it ring true?‚Äîthat freed him up, and he came to see that Pixar‚Äôs success was reliant on its movies connecting deeply with an audience. Given the way his behavior has been described in the past, you might think that giving constructive feedback to a vulnerable director on a not-baked-yet film would not be something that Steve could do gracefully, if at all. But in fact, over time, he became quite skilled at it. Pete Docter remembers Steve telling him once that he hoped in his next life he would come back as a Pixar director. I have no doubt that if he did, he‚Äôd be one of the best.&#13; As summer gave way to fall in 2003, Steve became increasingly hard to get ahold of. He was known for responding to emails, at all times of day and night, within minutes. But now, I would call or email and not hear back. In October, he dropped by Pixar, which was unusual‚Äîunless there was a board meeting, we usually briefed each other by phone. When John and I sat down with him, Steve closed the door and told us that he‚Äôd been having this aching in his back that wouldn‚Äôt go away. His doctor had recently sent him for a CAT scan, which revealed pancreatic cancer. Ninety-five percent of people with this diagnosis are not alive five years later, he told us. Steve was determined to fight, but he knew he might not win.&#13; Over the next eight years, Steve underwent a seemingly endless variety of treatments, both traditional and experimental. As his energy waned, our interactions became less frequent, though he still called weekly to check in, offer advice, and voice concerns. At one point during this period, John and I drove down to Apple to have lunch with him. Afterward, Steve took us into a secure room where Apple kept the supersecret products and showed us an early prototype of something he called the iPhone. It had a touch screen that engaged the user, making navigation not just easy but fun. We could instantly see that it made the phones we were carrying in our pockets look like ancient artifacts. He was particularly jazzed about it, he said, because it was his goal not just to create a phone people used but to design a phone people loved‚Äîone that made their lives better, both functionally and aesthetically. He thought Apple had succeeded in creating such a device.&#13; As we walked out of the vault, Steve stopped in the hallway and said he had been working on a list of three things he wanted to do‚Äîand I remember the words precisely‚Äî‚Äúbefore I sail away.‚Äù One goal that mattered enormously to him was to roll out the product he‚Äôd just shown us, along with a few others that he believed would ensure Apple‚Äôs future. The second was to safeguard Pixar‚Äôs continued success. And the third and most important was to set his three youngest children on a good path. I remember him saying that he hoped he would be around to watch his son Reed, then in 8th grade, graduate from high school. To hear this once-unstoppable man scaling back his hopes and ambitions to a handful of last wishes was heartbreaking, of course, but I remember thinking that when Steve said it, it sounded natural. It felt like he had come to terms with the inevitability of not being here.&#13; In the end, he would achieve all three of his goals.&#13; On a Sunday afternoon in February 2007, my daughter Jeannie and I stepped out of a town car, onto a long, red carpet ‚Ä¶ and ran smack into Steve Jobs. It was a few hours before the 79th Annual Academy Awards, and to get to our seats, the three of us had to plow through the crush of people outside the Kodak Theatre in the heart of Hollywood. Cars was nominated for Best Animated Feature Film, and, like all award hopefuls, we had a few preshow jitters. But as the three of us jostled along, Steve looked around at the circus‚Äîthe elegantly turned-out men and women, the scrum of television interviewers, the throngs of paparazzi and screaming onlookers, the line of limousines pulling up at the curb‚Äîand said, ‚ÄúWhat this scene really needs is a Buddhist monk lighting himself on fire.‚Äù&#13; Perspective is so hard to capture. I worked with Steve for more than a quarter-century‚Äîlonger, I believe, than anyone else‚Äîand I saw an arc to his life that does not accord with the one-note portraits of relentless perfectionism I‚Äôve read in magazines, newspapers, and even his own authorized biography. Relentless Steve‚Äîthe boorish, brilliant, but emotionally tone-deaf guy that we first came to know‚Äîchanged into a different man during the last two decades of his life. All of us who knew Steve well noticed the transformation. He became more sensitive not only to other people‚Äôs feelings but also to their value as contributors to the creative process.&#13; His experience with Pixar was part of this change. Steve aspired to create utilitarian things that also brought joy; it was his way of making the world a better place. That was part of why Pixar made him so proud‚Äîbecause he felt the world was better for the films we made. He used to say regularly that as brilliant as Apple products were, eventually they all ended up in landfills. Pixar movies, on the other hand, would live forever. He believed, as I do, that because they dig for deeper truths, our movies will endure, and he found beauty in that idea. John talks about ‚Äúthe nobility of entertaining people.‚Äù Steve understood this mission to his core, particularly toward the end of his life, and‚Äîknowing that entertaining wasn‚Äôt his primary skill set‚Äîhe felt lucky to have been involved in it.&#13; Pixar occupied a special place in Steve‚Äôs world, and his role evolved during our time together. In the early years, he was our benefactor, the one who paid the bills to keep the lights on. Later, he became our protector‚Äîa constructive critic internally but our fiercest defender to the outside. We had some trying times together, to be sure, but through those difficulties, we forged a rare bond. I‚Äôve always thought that Pixar was like a well-loved stepchild for Steve‚Äîconceived before he entered our lives, maybe, but still nurtured by him in our formative years. In the decade before his death, I watched Steve change Pixar even as Pixar changed him. I say this while acknowledging that no segment of one‚Äôs life can be divorced from the rest; Steve was, of course, always learning from his family and from his colleagues at Apple. But there was something special about the time he spent with us‚Äîenhanced, counterintuitively, by the fact that Pixar was his sideline. His wife and children, of course, were paramount, and Apple was his first and most heralded professional achievement; Pixar was a place he could relax a little and play. While he never lost his intensity, we watched him develop the ability to listen. More and more, he could express empathy and caring and patience. He became truly wise. The change in him was real, and it was deep.&#13; In chapter 5, I mentioned that, at my insistence, Steve didn‚Äôt attend Braintrust meetings. But he would often give notes after movies were screened for Pixar‚Äôs board of directors. Once or twice per movie, when a crisis loomed, he would inevitably come in and say something that helped alter our perceptions and improve the film. Whenever offering a note, he always began the same way: ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not really a filmmaker, so you can ignore everything I say.‚Ä¶ ‚Äù Then he would proceed, with startling efficiency, to diagnose the problem precisely. Steve focused on the problem itself, not the filmmakers, which made his critiques all the more powerful. If you sense a criticism is being leveled for personal reasons, it is easy to dismiss. You couldn‚Äôt dismiss Steve. Every film he commented on benefited from his insight.&#13; But while in the early days his opinions would swing wildly and his delivery could be abrupt, he became more articulate and observant of people‚Äôs feelings as time went on. He learned to read the room, demonstrating skills that, years earlier, I didn‚Äôt think he had. Some people have said that he got mellower with age, but I don‚Äôt think that‚Äôs an adequate description of what happened; it sounds too passive, as if he just was letting more go. Steve‚Äôs transformation was an active one. He continued to engage; he just changed the way he went about it.&#13; There is a phrase that many have used to describe Steve‚Äôs knack for accomplishing the impossible. Steve, they say, employed a ‚Äúreality distortion field.‚Äù In his biography of Steve, Walter Isaacson devoted an entire chapter to it, quoting Andy Hertzfeld, a member of the original Mac team at Apple, saying, ‚ÄúThe reality distortion field was a confounding m√©lange of a charismatic rhetorical style, indomitable will, and eagerness to bend any fact to fit the purpose at hand.‚Äù I heard the phrase used fairly often around Pixar, too. Some people, after listening to Steve, would feel that they had reached a new level of insight, only to find afterward that they could not reconstruct the steps in his reasoning; then the insight would evaporate, leaving them scratching their heads, feeling they had been led down the garden path. Thus, reality distortion.&#13; I disliked the phrase because it carried a whiff of negativity‚Äîimplying that Steve would try to will a fantasy world into being on a whim, without regard to how his refusal to face facts meant that everybody around him had to pull all-nighters and upend their lives in the hopes of meeting his unmeetable expectations. Much has been made of Steve‚Äôs refusal to follow rules‚Äîrealities‚Äîthat applied to others; famously, for example, he did not put a license plate on his car. But to focus too much on this is to miss something important. He recognized that many rules were in fact arbitrary. Yes, he tested boundaries and crossed the line at times. As a behavioral trait, that can be seen as antisocial‚Äîor if it happens to change the world, it can earn you the label ‚Äúvisionary.‚Äù We frequently support the idea of pushing boundaries in theory, ignoring the trouble it can cause in practice.&#13; Before Pixar was called Pixar, it was devoted to accomplishing something that had never been done before. For me, this had been a lifelong goal, and my colleagues at Pixar‚ÄîSteve among them‚Äîwere willing to make that leap, too, before computers had enough speed or memory to make it a reality. A characteristic of creative people is that they imagine making the impossible possible. That imagining‚Äîdreaming, noodling, audaciously rejecting what is (for the moment) true‚Äîis the way we discover what is new or important. Steve understood the value of science and law, but he also understood that complex systems respond in nonlinear, unpredictable ways. And that creativity, at its best, surprises us all.&#13; There is another, different meaning of reality distortion for me. It stems from my belief that our decisions and actions have consequences and that those consequences shape our future. Our actions change our reality. Our intentions matter. Most people believe that their actions have consequences but don‚Äôt think through the implications of that belief. But Steve did. He believed, as I do, that it is precisely by acting on our intentions and staying true to our values that we change the world.&#13; On August 24, 2011, Steve resigned as Apple‚Äôs CEO, as he was no longer able to keep pace with the rigors of the job he loved. Shortly thereafter, I was exercising at home early in the morning when the phone rang. It was Steve. To be honest, I can‚Äôt remember exactly what was said, because I knew he was nearing the end, and that was an incredibly difficult reality to deal with. But I recall that his voice was strong‚Äîstronger than it should have been, given what he‚Äôd been through‚Äîas he talked about how many years we‚Äôd worked together and how grateful he was to have had that experience. I remember him saying that he felt honored to have been a part of Pixar‚Äôs success. I told him I felt honored, too, and was thankful for his friendship, his example, and his loyalty. When we hung up, I said to myself, ‚ÄúThat was the goodbye call.‚Äù I was right: He would live six more weeks, but I would never hear his voice again.&#13; On a Monday morning five days after his death, the entire Pixar workforce gathered in the atrium of the building Steve had built to mourn and remember. By 11 A.M., the atrium was full of people, and it was time to begin. I stood off to the side, thinking about the man who‚Äôd been Pixar‚Äôs fiercest champion and a close friend. It fell on me to speak first.&#13; There were so many things I could say about Steve‚Äîhow he bought the division that would become Pixar from George Lucas in 1986, saving us from extinction; how he encouraged us to embark on our first feature film, Toy Story, three years later, when the idea of a computer-animated film still seemed beyond our reach; how he‚Äôd solidified our future by selling us to Disney and then ensured our autonomy by orchestrating a merger that created a true partnership; how he helped take us from forty-three employees to the 1,100 men and women who stood in front of me now. Looking back, I could recall the earliest moments of our relationship‚Äîhim probing and poking, me honing and fortifying my ideas. He had made me more focused, more resilient, smarter, better. Over time, I had come to rely on his demanding specificity, which never failed to help me clarify my own thinking. I could already feel the weight of his absence.&#13; ‚ÄúI remember twenty-five years ago in February, the day that Pixar was formed,‚Äù I began, recalling how we gathered in a conference room at Lucasfilm to sign the papers transferring majority ownership to Steve. We were exhausted, having spent months looking for potential suitors before Steve stepped forward. For those who weren‚Äôt at Pixar in the beginning, I recalled how Steve had pulled Alvy Ray Smith and me aside, put his arms around us, and said, ‚ÄúAs we‚Äôre going through this, there‚Äôs one thing I dearly ask. And that is that we be loyal to each other.‚Äù I told my colleagues that Steve had always made good on that promise. ‚ÄúOver the years, Pixar and Steve went through a lot of changes and a lot of hardships,‚Äù I said. ‚ÄúThese were very hard times. Pixar came close to collapsing. Any other investor or venture capitalist would have given up.‚Äù But not Steve. He demanded of himself what he‚Äôd asked of us: loyalty.&#13; ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know what happens in the future,‚Äù I concluded as the sun streamed through the skylights above us. ‚ÄúBut I do believe that Steve‚Äôs focus on passion and quality will take us places that we cannot yet perceive. And for that I am truly grateful.‚Äù At that moment, I was more aware than ever of how important it was to understand and protect what had made Steve so proud. It had always been my goal to create a culture at Pixar that would outlast its leaders‚ÄîSteve, John, and me. Now one of us had taken his leave too soon, and the job of fortifying that culture‚Äîensuring that it would be self-sustaining‚Äîwas left to John and me.&#13; When I was done, I offered the microphone to others who‚Äôd had a close relationship with Steve and, one by one, they stepped onto the podium. Andrew Stanton described Steve as ‚Äúthe creative firewall.‚Äù With Steve around, the people of Pixar ‚Äúwere like free-range chickens,‚Äù he said, getting a laugh. ‚ÄúSteve would do anything to keep us creatively safe.‚Äù&#13; The ever-observant Pete Docter got up next and recalled one of the most endearing images he had of Steve. During a meeting one day years ago, Pete noticed that Steve had two small, identical holes in one of the legs of his Levis 501s. Steve shifted in his seat, and Pete noticed the same two holes on the other leg, too, in the same spot, right above the ankle. As Pete was trying‚Äîand failing‚Äîto imagine a reason for these symmetrical holes, Steve reached down to pull up his socks by grabbing them through his pants‚Äîputting his fingers right where the holes were! ‚ÄúHere Steve was worth millions, but apparently getting a new pair of pants was not important to him,‚Äù Pete said. ‚ÄúOr maybe he needed new socks with better elastic. Either way, it was a humanizing aspect to this larger-than-life guy.‚Äù&#13; Brad Bird recalled that when he first started talking to Pixar about doing The Incredibles, he wasn‚Äôt sure if he would take the offer: He was still considering staying with Warner Bros., which had released his earlier movie, The Iron Giant. ‚ÄúBut it took me a month to get a meeting with the administration of the studio I‚Äôd just made a movie for,‚Äù Brad said. ‚ÄúAnd in the meantime, Steve knew the name of my wife, asked how my kids were by name‚Äîhe did his homework. I thought, ‚ÄòWhat the hell am I doing talking to Warner Bros.?‚Äô It cinched the deal.‚Äù&#13; ‚ÄúSteve held the bar for quality,‚Äù Brad continued. ‚ÄúHe was always about the long run. He was into Buddhism, but I see him more as just a spiritual guy. I have to believe that he believed in something beyond this‚Äù‚Äîhe hesitated, overcome for a moment‚Äî‚Äúand that‚Äôs where we‚Äôll see him again. Where cream rises to the top. So here‚Äôs to you, Steve, and to the long run.‚Äù&#13; It was John‚Äôs turn now. The room fell silent, but you could feel the current of emotion around us all. Stepping to the podium, he described what an honor it had been to be Steve‚Äôs friend as he changed‚Äîlike we all aspire to do‚Äîfor the better.&#13; ‚ÄúWhen Steve first bought us,‚Äù John said, ‚Äúthere was a confidence he had. Some people call it arrogance; I call it confidence. But it was basically a belief that he could do anybody‚Äôs job better than they could. That‚Äôs why people hated getting into an elevator at Apple with Steve because they felt by the time they got to the top floor, they‚Äôd probably be fired.‚Äù Again, the room broke out in laughter. ‚ÄúBut as Pixar evolved into an animation studio, he started to look at all the work that we were doing, and he was amazed. He realized he couldn‚Äôt even come close to doing what we could do. I like to think that when he was building Pixar, when he and Laurene got married and he had his kids, that that realization of how brilliant the people here at Pixar were‚Äîthat all this helped make him the amazing leader he was.‚Äù&#13; Three weeks before, John had visited Steve for the last time. ‚ÄúWe sat for about an hour talking about coming projects he was so interested in,‚Äù John said, his voice catching. ‚ÄúI looked at him and I realized this man had given me‚Äîgiven us‚Äîeverything that we could ever want. I gave him a big hug. I kissed him on the cheek and for all of you‚Äù‚ÄîJohn was crying now‚Äî‚ÄúI said, ‚ÄòThank you. I love you, Steve.‚Äô ‚Äù&#13; The room erupted in applause, which only ebbed when one of the Pixar Singers took the stage. In a quiet voice, he announced that just as our resident a capella group had sung at every Pixar wrap party in our company‚Äôs history, it would now sing for Steve, too. Standing in the building that we all called ‚ÄúSteve‚Äôs movie,‚Äù I couldn‚Äôt help but think that he would have loved this‚Äîa fitting wrap to the production that was Steve Jobs.&#13; The roller coaster came to a stop and a good friend got off, but what a ride we‚Äôd taken together. It had been one hell of a trip.&#13;

 STARTING POINTS&#13; &#13; THOUGHTS FOR MANAGING A CREATIVE CULTURE&#13; &#13; Here are some of the principles we‚Äôve developed over the years to enable and protect a healthy creative culture. I know that when you distill a complex idea into a T-shirt slogan, you risk giving the illusion of understanding‚Äîand, in the process, of sapping the idea of its power. An adage worth repeating is also halfway to being irrelevant. You end up with something that is easy to say but not connected to behavior. But while I have been dismissive of reductive truths throughout this book, I do have a point of view, and I thought it might be helpful to share some of the principles that I hold most dear here with you. The trick is to think of each statement as a starting point, as a prompt toward deeper inquiry, and not as a conclusion.&#13; &#13; ‚Ä¢ Give a good idea to a mediocre team, and they will screw it up. Give a mediocre idea to a great team, and they will either fix it or come up with something better. If you get the team right, chances are that they‚Äôll get the ideas right.&#13; ‚Ä¢ When looking to hire people, give their potential to grow more weight than their current skill level. What they will be capable of tomorrow is more important than what they can do today.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Always try to hire people who are smarter than you. Always take a chance on better, even if it seems like a potential threat.&#13; ‚Ä¢ If there are people in your organization who feel they are not free to suggest ideas, you lose. Do not discount ideas from unexpected sources. Inspiration can, and does, come from anywhere.&#13; ‚Ä¢ It isn‚Äôt enough merely to be open to ideas from others. Engaging the collective brainpower of the people you work with is an active, ongoing process. As a manager, you must coax ideas out of your staff and constantly push them to contribute.&#13; ‚Ä¢ There are many valid reasons why people aren‚Äôt candid with one another in a work environment. Your job is to search for those reasons and then address them.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Likewise, if someone disagrees with you, there is a reason. Our first job is to understand the reasoning behind their conclusions.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Further, if there is fear in an organization, there is a reason for it‚Äîour job is (a) to find what‚Äôs causing it, (b) to understand it, and (c) to try to root it out.&#13; ‚Ä¢ There is nothing quite as effective, when it comes to shutting down alternative viewpoints, as being convinced you are right.&#13; ‚Ä¢ In general, people are hesitant to say things that might rock the boat. Braintrust meetings, dailies, postmortems, and Notes Day are all efforts to reinforce the idea that it is okay to express yourself. All are mechanisms of self-assessment that seek to uncover what‚Äôs real.&#13; ‚Ä¢ If there is more truth in the hallways than in meetings, you have a problem.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Many managers feel that if they are not notified about problems before others are or if they are surprised in a meeting, then that is a sign of disrespect. Get over it.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Careful ‚Äúmessaging‚Äù to downplay problems makes you appear to be lying, deluded, ignorant, or uncaring. Sharing problems is an act of inclusion that makes employees feel invested in the larger enterprise.&#13; ‚Ä¢ The first conclusions we draw from our successes and failures are typically wrong. Measuring the outcome without evaluating the process is deceiving.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Do not fall for the illusion that by preventing errors, you won‚Äôt have errors to fix. The truth is, the cost of preventing errors is often far greater than the cost of fixing them.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Change and uncertainty are part of life. Our job is not to resist them but to build the capability to recover when unexpected events occur. If you don‚Äôt always try to uncover what is unseen and understand its nature, you will be ill prepared to lead.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Similarly, it is not the manager‚Äôs job to prevent risks. It is the manager‚Äôs job to make it safe to take them.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Failure isn‚Äôt a necessary evil. In fact, it isn‚Äôt evil at all. It is a necessary consequence of doing something new.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Trust doesn‚Äôt mean that you trust that someone won‚Äôt screw up‚Äîit means you trust them even when they do screw up.&#13; ‚Ä¢ The people ultimately responsible for implementing a plan must be empowered to make decisions when things go wrong, even before getting approval. Finding and fixing problems is everybody‚Äôs job. Anyone should be able to stop the production line.&#13; ‚Ä¢ The desire for everything to run smoothly is a false goal‚Äîit leads to measuring people by the mistakes they make rather than by their ability to solve problems.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt wait for things to be perfect before you share them with others. Show early and show often. It‚Äôll be pretty when we get there, but it won‚Äôt be pretty along the way. And that‚Äôs as it should be.&#13; ‚Ä¢ A company‚Äôs communication structure should not mirror its organizational structure. Everybody should be able to talk to anybody.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Be wary of making too many rules. Rules can simplify life for managers, but they can be demeaning to the 95 percent who behave well. Don‚Äôt create rules to rein in the other 5 percent‚Äîaddress abuses of common sense individually. This is more work but ultimately healthier.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Imposing limits can encourage a creative response. Excellent work can emerge from uncomfortable or seemingly untenable circumstances.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Engaging with exceptionally hard problems forces us to think differently.&#13; ‚Ä¢ An organization, as a whole, is more conservative and resistant to change than the individuals who comprise it. Do not assume that general agreement will lead to change‚Äîit takes substantial energy to move a group, even when all are on board.&#13; ‚Ä¢ The healthiest organizations are made up of departments whose agendas differ but whose goals are interdependent. If one agenda wins, we all lose.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Our job as managers in creative environments is to protect new ideas from those who don‚Äôt understand that in order for greatness to emerge, there must be phases of not-so-greatness. Protect the future, not the past.&#13; ‚Ä¢ New crises are not always lamentable‚Äîthey test and demonstrate a company‚Äôs values. The process of problem-solving often bonds people together and keeps the culture in the present.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Excellence, quality, and good should be earned words, attributed by others to us, not proclaimed by us about ourselves.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Do not accidentally make stability a goal. Balance is more important than stability.&#13; ‚Ä¢ Don‚Äôt confuse the process with the goal. Working on our processes to make them better, easier, and more efficient is an indispensable activity and something we should continually work on‚Äîbut it is not the goal. Making the product great is the goal.&#13; &#13;
